886 <section id="_1">

887

888 <h2>

889General Information About Rectal Cancer</h2>

890

891 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

892<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

893<h6>

894In This Section</h6>

895<ul>

896<li>

897<a href="#_256_toc">

898Incidence and Mortality</a>

899</li>

900<li>

901<a href="#_1079_toc">

902Anatomy</a>

903</li>

904<li>

905<a href="#_262_toc">

906Risk Factors</a>

907</li>

908<li>

909<a href="#_835_toc">

910Screening</a>

911</li>

912<li>

914Clinical Features</a>

915</li>

916<li>

918Diagnostic Evaluation</a>

919</li>

920<li>

922Prognostic Factors</a>

923</li>

924<li>

925<a href="#_297_toc">

926Follow-Up After Treatment<ul>

927<li>

928<a href="#_850_toc">

929Carcinoembryonic antigen (CEA)</a>

930</li>

931</ul>

932</a>

933</li>

934</ul>

935</nav>

936<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

937<a href="/types/colorectal/patient/rectal-treatment-pdq">

938Go to Patient Version</a>

939</div>

940<section id="_256">

941<h3 id="_256_toc">

942Incidence and Mortality</h3>

943<p id="_1005" tabindex="-1">

944It is difficult to separate epidemiological considerations of rectal cancer from those of colon cancer because studies often consider colon and rectal cancer together (i.e., colorectal cancer).</p>

945<p id="_846" tabindex="-1">

946Worldwide, colorectal cancer is the third most common form of cancer. In 2020, there were an estimated 1.93 million new cases of colorectal cancer and 935,173 deaths.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9471</a>

948]</p>

949<p id="_257" tabindex="-1">

950Estimated new cases and deaths from rectal and colon cancer in the United States in 2023:[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9512</a>

952]</p>

953<div class="pdq-content-list">

954<ul id="_258">

955<li>

956New cases of rectal cancer: 46,050.</li>

957<li>

958New cases of colon cancer: 106,970.</li>

959<li>

960Deaths: 52,550 (rectal and colon cancers combined).</li>

961</ul>

962</div>

963<p id="_1006" tabindex="-1">

964Colorectal cancer affects men and women almost equally. Among all racial groups in the United States, Black individuals have the highest sporadic colorectal cancer incidence and mortality rates.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9653</a>

966,<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9674</a>

968]</p>

969</section>

970<section id="_1079">

971<h3 id="_1079_toc">

972Anatomy</h3>

973<p id="_1068" tabindex="-1">

974<figure id="figure_1069" class="image-center-medium">

975<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/415499.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

976Enlarge</a>

977<img id="_1069" alt="Gastrointestinal (digestive) system anatomy; drawing shows the esophagus, liver, stomach, colon, small intestine, rectum, and anus." title="Gastrointestinal (digestive) system anatomy; drawing shows the esophagus, liver, stomach, colon, small intestine, rectum, and anus." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/415499-750.jpg">

978<figcaption class="caption-container">

979Anatomy of the lower gastrointestinal (digestive) system.</figcaption>

980</figure>

981</p>

982<p id="_325" tabindex="-1">

983The rectum is located within the pelvis, extending from the transitional mucosa of the anal dentate line to the sigmoid colon at the peritoneal reflection. By rigid sigmoidoscopy, the rectum measures between 10 cm and 15 cm from the anal verge.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

9845</a>

985] The location of a rectal tumor is usually indicated by the distance between the anal verge, dentate line, or anorectal ring and the lower edge of the tumor, with measurements differing depending on the use of a rigid or flexible endoscope or digital examination.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

9866</a>

987] </p>

988<p id="_1098" tabindex="-1">

989The distance of the tumor from the anal sphincter musculature has implications for the ability to perform sphincter-sparing surgery. The bony constraints of the pelvis limit surgical access to the rectum, which results in a lower likelihood of attaining widely negative margins and a higher risk of local recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

9905</a>

991]</p>

992</section>

993<section id="_262">

994<h3 id="_262_toc">

995Risk Factors</h3>

996<section id="_sm_CDR0000752412_1">

997<p id="_sm_CDR0000752412_4" tabindex="-1">

998Increasing age is the most important risk factor for most cancers. Other risk factors for colorectal cancer include the following:</p>

999<div class="pdq-content-list">

1000<ul id="_sm_CDR0000752412_2">

1001<li>

1002Family history of colorectal cancer in a first-degree relative.[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

10037</a>

1004] </li>

1005<li>

1006Personal history of colorectal adenomas, colorectal cancer, or ovarian cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

10078</a>

1008-<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

100910</a>

1010]</li>

1011<li>

1012Hereditary conditions, including familial adenomatous polyposis (FAP) and Lynch syndrome (hereditary nonpolyposis colorectal cancer [HNPCC]).[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

101311</a>

1014]</li>

1015<li>

1016Personal history of long-standing chronic ulcerative colitis or Crohn colitis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

101712</a>

1018]</li>

1019<li>

1020Excessive alcohol use.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

102113</a>

1022]</li>

1023<li>

1024Cigarette smoking.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

102514</a>

1026]</li>

1027<li>

1028Race and ethnicity: African American.[<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

102915</a>

1030,<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

103116</a>

1032]</li>

1033<li>

1034Obesity.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

103517</a>

1036]</li>

1037</ul>

1038</div>

1039</section>

1040</section>

1041<section id="_835">

1042<h3 id="_835_toc">

1043Screening</h3>

1044<p id="_836" tabindex="-1">

1045Evidence supports screening for rectal cancer as a part of routine care for all adults aged 50 years and older, especially for those with first-degree relatives with colorectal cancer. Reasons include the following:</p>

1046<div class="pdq-content-list">

1047<ul id="_837">

1048<li>

1049Incidence of the disease in adults 50 years and older.</li>

1050<li>

1051Ability to identify high-risk groups.</li>

1052<li>

1053Slow growth of primary lesions.</li>

1054<li>

1055Better survival of patients with early-stage lesions.</li>

1056<li>

1057Relative simplicity and accuracy of screening tests.</li>

1058</ul>

1059</div>

1060<p id="_838" tabindex="-1">

1061For more information, see <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/colorectal-screening-pdq">

1062Colorectal Cancer Screening</a>

1063.</p>

1064</section>

1065<section id="_279">

1066<h3 id="_279_toc">

1067Clinical Features</h3>

1068<p id="_280" tabindex="-1">

1069Similar to colon cancer, symptoms of rectal cancer may include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

107018</a>

1071]</p>

1072<div class="pdq-content-list">

1073<ul id="_1084">

1074<li>

1075Rectal bleeding.</li>

1076<li>

1077Change in bowel habits.</li>

1078<li>

1079Abdominal pain.</li>

1080<li>

1081Intestinal obstruction.</li>

1082<li>

1083Change in appetite.</li>

1084<li>

1085Weight loss.</li>

1086<li>

1087Weakness.</li>

1088</ul>

1089</div>

1090<p id="_327" tabindex="-1">

1091With the exception of obstructive symptoms, these symptoms do not necessarily correlate with the stage of disease or signify a particular diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

109219</a>

1093] </p>

1094</section>

1095<section id="_281">

1096<h3 id="_281_toc">

1097Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

1098<p id="_629" tabindex="-1">

1099The initial clinical evaluation may include the following:</p>

1100<div class="pdq-content-list">

1101<ul id="_630">

1102<li>

1103Physical exam and history.</li>

1104<li>

1105Digital rectal exam.</li>

1106<li>

1107Colonoscopy.</li>

1108<li>

1109Biopsy.</li>

1110<li>

1111Carcinoembryonic antigen (CEA) assay.</li>

1112<li>

1113Immunohistochemistry.</li>

1114<li>

1115DNA mismatch repair/microsatellite instability (MSI) testing.</li>

1116</ul>

1117</div>

1118<p id="_849" tabindex="-1">

1119Physical examination may reveal a palpable mass and bright blood in the rectum. Adenopathy, hepatomegaly, or pulmonary signs may be present with metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11206</a>

1121] Laboratory examination may reveal iron-deficiency anemia and electrolyte and liver function abnormalities. </p>

1122</section>

1123<section id="_293">

1124<h3 id="_293_toc">

1125Prognostic Factors</h3>

1126<p id="_294" tabindex="-1">

1127The prognosis of patients with rectal cancer is related to several factors, including the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11286</a>

1129,<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

113020</a>

1131-<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

113228</a>

1133]</p>

1134<div class="pdq-content-list">

1135<ul id="_295">

1136<li>

1137Tumor adherence to or invasion of adjacent organs.[<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

113820</a>

1139] </li>

1140<li>

1141Presence or absence of tumor involvement in the lymph nodes and the number of positive lymph nodes.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11426</a>

1143,<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

114421</a>

1145-<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

114624</a>

1147]</li>

1148<li>

1149 Presence or absence of distant metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11506</a>

1151,<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

115220</a>

1153] </li>

1154<li>

1155Perforation or obstruction of the bowel.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11566</a>

1157,<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

115828</a>

1159]</li>

1160<li>

1161Presence or absence of high-risk pathological features, including the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

116226</a>

1163,<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

116427</a>

1165,<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

116629</a>

1167]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1168<ul id="_855" class="list-dash">

1169<li>

1170Positive surgical margins.</li>

1171<li>

1172Lymphovascular invasion.</li>

1173<li>

1174Perineural invasion.</li>

1175<li>

1176Poorly differentiated histology.</li>

1177</ul>

1178</div>

1179</li>

1180<li>

1181Circumferential resection margin (CRM) or depth of penetration of the tumor through the bowel wall.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11826</a>

1183,<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

118425</a>

1185,<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

118630</a>

1187] Measured in millimeters, CRM is defined as the retroperitoneal or peritoneal adventitial soft-tissue margin closest to the deepest penetration of the tumor. </li>

1188<li>

1189Presence of MSI that results from impaired DNA mismatch repair.</li>

1190</ul>

1191</div>

1192<p id="_416" tabindex="-1">

1193Only disease stage (designated by tumor [T], nodal status [N], and distant metastasis [M]) has been validated as a prognostic factor in multi-institutional prospective studies.[<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

119420</a>

1195-<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

119625</a>

1197] A major pooled analysis evaluating the impact of T and N stage and treatment on survival and relapse in patients with rectal cancer who are treated with adjuvant therapy confirmed these findings.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

119831</a>

1199]</p>

1200<p id="_296" tabindex="-1">

1201Mismatch repair deficiency occurs in 5% to 10% of patients with rectal adenocarcinomas. Mismatch repair&#8211;deficient tumors do not respond well to chemotherapy applied in the neoadjuvant, adjuvant, or metastatic settings.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

120232</a>

1203-<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

120434</a>

1205] In a population-based series of 607 patients aged 50 years or younger at the time of diagnosis, MSI-related colorectal cancer was associated with improved survival that was independent of tumor stage. MSI is also associated with Lynch syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

120635</a>

1207] In addition, gene expression profiling is useful for predicting the response of rectal adenocarcinomas to preoperative chemoradiation therapy. It can also help determine the prognosis of stages II and III rectal cancer after neoadjuvant fluorouracil-based chemoradiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

120836</a>

1209,<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

121037</a>

1211] </p>

1212<p id="_806" tabindex="-1">

1213Racial and ethnic differences in overall survival (OS) after adjuvant therapy for rectal cancer have been observed, with shorter OS for Black patients than for White patients. Factors contributing to this disparity may include tumor position, type of surgical procedure, and presence of comorbid conditions.[<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

121438</a>

1215]</p>

1216</section>

1217<section id="_297">

1218<h3 id="_297_toc">

1219Follow-Up After Treatment</h3>

1220<p id="_298" tabindex="-1">

1221The primary goals of postoperative surveillance programs for rectal cancer are to:[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

122239</a>

1223]</p>

1224<div class="pdq-content-list">

1225<ol id="_1080">

1226<li>

1227Assess the efficacy of initial therapy.</li>

1228<li>

1229Detect new or metachronous malignancies.</li>

1230<li>

1231Detect potentially curable recurrent or metastatic cancers.</li>

1232</ol>

1233</div>

1234<p id="_1081" tabindex="-1">

1235Routine, periodic studies following treatment for rectal cancer may lead to earlier identification and management of recurrent disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

123639</a>

1237-<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

123843</a>

1239] A statistically significant survival benefit has been demonstrated for more intensive follow-up protocols in two clinical trials. A meta-analysis that combined these two trials with four others reported a statistically significant improvement in survival for patients who were intensively followed.[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

124039</a>

1241,<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

124244</a>

1243,<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

124445</a>

1245]</p>

1246<p id="_807" tabindex="-1">

1247Guidelines for surveillance after initial treatment

1248with curative intent for colorectal cancer vary between leading U.S. and European oncology societies, and optimal surveillance strategies remain uncertain.[<a href="#cit/section_1.46">

124946</a>

1250,<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

125147</a>

1252] Large, well-designed, prospective, multi-institutional, randomized studies are required to establish an evidence-based consensus for follow-up evaluation.</p>

1253<section id="_850">

1254<h4 id="_850_toc">

1255Carcinoembryonic antigen (CEA)</h4>

1256<p id="_851" tabindex="-1">

1257Measurement of CEA, a serum glycoprotein, is frequently used in the management and follow-up of patients with rectal cancer. A review of the use of this tumor marker for rectal cancer suggests the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

125839</a>

1259]</p>

1260<div class="pdq-content-list">

1261<ul id="_852">

1262<li>

1263Serum CEA testing is not a valuable screening tool for rectal cancer because of its low sensitivity and low specificity.</li>

1264<li>

1265Postoperative CEA testing is typically restricted to patients who are potential candidates for further intervention, as follows:<div class="pdq-content-list">

1266<ul id="_1109" class="list-dash">

1267<li>

1268Patients with stage II or III rectal cancer (every 2&#8211;3 months for at least 2 years after diagnosis).</li>

1269<li>

1270Patients with rectal cancer who would be candidates for resection of liver metastases.</li>

1271</ul>

1272</div>

1273</li>

1274</ul>

1275</div>

1276<p id="_854" tabindex="-1">

1277In one Dutch retrospective study of total mesorectal excision for the treatment of rectal cancer, investigators found that the preoperative serum CEA level was normal in most patients with rectal cancer, and yet, serum CEA levels rose by at least 50% in patients with recurrence. The authors concluded that serial, postoperative CEA testing cannot be discarded based on a normal preoperative serum CEA level in patients with rectal cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

127848</a>

1279,<a href="#cit/section_1.49">

128049</a>

1281]</p>

1282</section>

1283</section>

1506<p id="_856" tabindex="-1">

1507Adenocarcinomas account for most rectal tumors in the United States. Other histological types account for an estimated 2% to 5% of colorectal tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

15081</a>

1509]</p>

1511The World Health Organization classification of tumors of the colon and rectum includes the following:[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

15122</a>

1513]</p>

1648 <section id="_19">

1649

1650 <h2>

1651Stage Information for Rectal Cancer</h2>

1652

1653 <div id="_section_19" class="pdq-sections">

1654<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1655<h6>

1656In This Section</h6>

1657<ul>

1658<li>

1659<a href="#_633_toc">

1660Staging Evaluation</a>

1661</li>

1662<li>

1663<a href="#_638_toc">

1664AJCC Stage Groupings and TNM Definitions<ul>

1665<li>

1666<a href="#_1012_toc">

1667Lymph node status</a>

1668</li>

1669</ul>

1670</a>

1671</li>

1672</ul>

1673</nav>

1674<p id="_632" tabindex="-1">

1675Accurate staging provides crucial information about the location and size of the primary tumor in the rectum, and, if present, the size, number, and location of any metastases. Accurate initial staging can influence therapy by helping to determine the type of surgical intervention and the choice of neoadjuvant therapy to maximize the likelihood of resection with clear margins. In primary rectal cancer, pelvic imaging helps determine the following:[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

16761</a>

1677-<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

16787</a>

1679]</p>

1680<div class="pdq-content-list">

1681<ul id="_839">

1682<li>

1683The depth of tumor invasion.</li>

1684<li>

1685The distance from the sphincter complex.</li>

1686<li>

1687The potential for achieving negative circumferential (radial) margins.</li>

1688<li>

1689The involvement of locoregional lymph nodes or adjacent organs.</li>

1690</ul>

1691</div>

1692<section id="_633">

1693<h3 id="_633_toc">

1694Staging Evaluation</h3>

1695<p id="_634" tabindex="-1">

1696Clinical evaluation and staging procedures may include the following:</p>

1697<div class="pdq-content-list">

1698<ul id="_635">

1699<li>

1700<strong>

1701Digital-rectal examination (DRE):</strong>

1702 DRE and/or rectovaginal exam and rigid proctoscopy to determine if sphincter-saving surgery is possible.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

17031</a>

1704,<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

17052</a>

1706,<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

17075</a>

1708]</li>

1709<li>

1710<strong>

1711Colonoscopy:</strong>

1712 Complete colonoscopy to rule out cancers elsewhere in the bowel.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

17135</a>

1714]</li>

1715<li>

1716<strong>

1717Computed tomography (CT):</strong>

1718 Pan-body CT scan to rule out metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

17195</a>

1720]</li>

1721<li>

1722<strong>

1723Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI):</strong>

1724 MRI of the abdomen and pelvis to determine the depth of penetration and the potential for achieving negative circumferential (radial) margins and to identify locoregional nodal metastases and distant metastatic disease. MRI may be particularly helpful in determining sacral involvement in local recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

17251</a>

1726]</li>

1727<li>

1728<strong>

1729 Endorectal ultrasound:</strong>

1730 Endorectal ultrasound with a rigid probe or a flexible scope for stenotic lesions to determine the depth of penetration and identify locoregional nodal metastases.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

17312</a>

1732,<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

17334</a>

1734]</li>

1735<li>

1736<strong>

1737 Positron emission tomography (PET):</strong>

1738 PET to image distant metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

17391</a>

1740]</li>

1741<li>

1742<strong>

1743Carcinoembryonic antigen (CEA):</strong>

1744 Measurement of the serum CEA level for prognostic assessment and the determination of response to therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

17456</a>

1746,<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

17477</a>

1748]</li>

1749</ul>

1750</div>

1751<p id="_636" tabindex="-1">

1752In the tumor (T) staging of rectal carcinoma, several studies indicate that the accuracy of endorectal ultrasound ranges from 80% to 95% compared with 65% to 75% for CT and 75% to 85% for MRI. The accuracy in determining metastatic nodal involvement by endorectal ultrasound is approximately 70% to 75% compared with 55% to 65% for CT and 60% to 70% for MRI.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

17532</a>

1754] In a meta-analysis of 84 studies, none of the three imaging modalities, including endorectal ultrasound, CT, and MRI, were significantly superior to the others in staging nodal (N) status.[<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

17558</a>

1756] Endorectal ultrasound using a rigid probe may be similarly accurate in T and N staging when compared with endorectal ultrasound using a flexible scope. However, a technically difficult endorectal ultrasound may give an inconclusive or inaccurate result for both T stage and N stage. In this case, further assessment by MRI or flexible endorectal ultrasound may be considered.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

17574</a>

1758,<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

17599</a>

1760]</p>

1761<p id="_637" tabindex="-1">

1762In patients with rectal cancer, the circumferential resection margin is an important pathological staging parameter. Measured in millimeters, it is defined as the retroperitoneal or peritoneal adventitial soft-tissue margin closest to the deepest penetration of tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

176310</a>

1764]</p>

1765</section>

1766<section id="_638">

1767<h3 id="_638_toc">

1768AJCC Stage Groupings and TNM Definitions</h3>

1769<p id="_643" tabindex="-1">

1770The AJCC has designated staging by TNM (tumor, node, metastasis) classification to define rectal cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

177111</a>

1772] The same classification is used for both clinical and pathological staging.[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

177311</a>

1774] Treatment decisions are made with reference to the TNM classification system, rather than the older Dukes or Modified Astler-Coller classification

1775schema.

1776</p>

1777<p id="_1010" tabindex="-1">

1778Cancers staged using this staging system include adenocarcinomas, high-grade neuroendocrine carcinomas, and squamous carcinomas of the colon and rectum. Cancers not staged using this staging system include these histopathological types of cancer: appendiceal carcinomas, anal carcinomas, well-differentiated neuroendocrine tumors (carcinoids).[<a href="#cit/section_3.11">

177911</a>

1780] For more information, see <a href="/types/anal/hp/anal-treatment-pdq">

1781Anal Cancer Treatment</a>

1782 and <a href="/types/gi-neuroendocrine-tumors/hp/gi-neuroendocrine-treatment-pdq">

1783Gastrointestinal Neuroendocrine Tumors Treatment</a>

1784.</p>

1785<section id="_1012">

1786<h4 id="_1012_toc">

1787Lymph node status</h4>

1788<p id="_1013" tabindex="-1">

1789The AJCC and a National Cancer Institute-sponsored panel suggested that at least 10 to 14 lymph nodes be examined in radical colon and rectum resections in patients who did not receive neoadjuvant therapy. In cases in which a tumor is resected for palliation or in patients who have received preoperative radiation therapy, fewer lymph nodes may be present.[<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

179010</a>

1791-<a href="#cit/section_3.12">

179212</a>

1793] This takes into consideration that the number of lymph nodes examined is a reflection of both the aggressiveness of lymphovascular mesenteric dissection at the time of surgical resection and the pathological identification of nodes in the specimen. </p>

1794<p id="_1014" tabindex="-1">

1795Retrospective studies, such as Intergroup trial <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00201331">

1796INT-0089</a>

1797 (NCT00201331), have demonstrated that the number of lymph nodes examined during colon and rectal surgery may be associated with patient outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_3.13">

179813</a>

1799-<a href="#cit/section_3.16">

180016</a>

1801]</p>

1802<p id="_1212" tabindex="-1">

1803A new tumor-metastasis staging strategy for node-positive rectal cancer has been proposed.[<a href="#cit/section_3.17">

180417</a>

1805]

2347</tr>

2348<tr>

2349<td colspan="4">

2350<span class="sup">

2351b </span>

2352Direct invasion in T4 includes invasion of other organs or other segments of the colorectum as a result of direct extension through the serosa, as confirmed on microscopic examination (e.g., invasion of the sigmoid colon by a carcinoma of the cecum) or, for cancers in a retroperitoneal or subperitoneal location, direct invasion of other organs or structures by virtue of extension beyond the muscularis propria (i.e., respectively, a tumor on the posterior wall of the descending colon invading the left kidney or lateral abdominal wall; or a mid or distal rectal cancer with invasion of prostate, seminal vesicles, cervix, or vagina). </td>

2353</tr>

2354<tr>

2355<td colspan="4">

2356<span class="sup">

2357c</span>

2358Tumor that is adherent to other organs or structures, grossly, is classified cT4b. However, if no tumor is present in the adhesion, microscopically, the classification should be pT1-4a depending on the anatomical depth of wall invasion. The V and L classification should be used to identify the presence or absence of vascular or lymphatic invasion whereas the PN prognostic factor should be used for perineural invasion.</td>

2359</tr>

2360</TFoot>

2361<tbody>

2362<tr>

2363<td rowspan="19">

2364IVA</td>

2365<td rowspan="19">

2366Any T, Any N, M1a</td>

2367<td>

2368TX = Primary tumor cannot be assessed.</td>

2369<td rowspan="25">

2370<figure id="figure_1253" class="image-center">

2371<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/694405.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

2372Enlarge</a>

2373<img id="_1253" alt="Stage IV rectal cancer; drawing shows other parts of the body where rectal cancer may spread, including the distant lymph nodes, lung, liver, abdominal wall, and prostate. An inset shows cancer cells spreading from the rectum, through the blood and lymph system, to another part of the body where metastatic cancer has formed." title="Stage IV rectal cancer; drawing shows other parts of the body where rectal cancer may spread, including the distant lymph nodes, lung, liver, abdominal wall, and prostate. An inset shows cancer cells spreading from the rectum, through the blood and lymph system, to another part of the body where metastatic cancer has formed." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/694405-750.jpg">

2374</figure>

2375</td>

2376</tr>

2377<tr>

2378<td>

2379T0 = No evidence of primary tumor.</td>

2380</tr>

2381<tr>

2382<td>

2383Tis = Carcinoma <em>

2384in situ</em>

2385, intramucosal carcinoma (involvement of lamina propria with no extension through muscularis mucosae).</td>

2386</tr>

2387<tr>

2388<td>

2389T1 = Tumor invades the submucosa (through the muscularis mucosa but not into the muscularis propria).</td>

2390</tr>

2391<tr>

2392<td>

2393T2 = Tumor invades the muscularis propria.</td>

2394</tr>

2395<tr>

2396<td>

2397T3 = Tumor invades through the muscularis propria into pericolorectal tissues.</td>

2398</tr>

2399<tr>

2400<td>

2401T4 = Tumor invades the visceral peritoneum or invades or adheres to adjacent organ or structure.</td>

2402</tr>

2403<tr>

2404<td>

2405&#8211;T4a = Tumor invades through the visceral peritoneum (including gross perforation of the bowel through tumor and continuous invasion of tumor through areas of inflammation to the surface of the visceral peritoneum).</td>

2406</tr>

2407<tr>

2408<td>

2409&#8211;T4b = Tumor directly invades or adheres to adjacent organs or structures.</td>

2410</tr>

2561 <section id="_43">

2562

2563 <h2>

2564Treatment Option Overview for Rectal Cancer</h2>

2565

2566 <div id="_section_43" class="pdq-sections">

2567<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2568<h6>

2569In This Section</h6>

2570<ul>

2571<li>

2574</li>

2599</a>

2600</li>

2601</ul>

2602</nav>

2603<p id="_809" tabindex="-1">

2604The management of rectal cancer varies somewhat from that of colon cancer because of the increased risk of local recurrence and a poorer overall prognosis. Differences include surgical technique, the use of radiation therapy, and the method of chemotherapy administration. In addition to determining the intent of rectal cancer surgery (i.e., curative or palliative), it is important to consider therapeutic issues related to the maintenance or restoration of normal anal sphincter, genitourinary function, and sexual function.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

26051</a>

2606,<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

26072</a>

2608]</p>

2609<p id="_810" tabindex="-1">

2610The approach to the management of rectal cancer is multimodal and involves a multidisciplinary team of cancer specialists with expertise in gastroenterology, medical oncology, surgical oncology, radiation oncology, and radiology.</p>

2749<section id="_1312">

2750<h3 id="_1312_toc">

2751Immunotherapy</h3>

2752<p id="_1313" tabindex="-1">

2753Among patients with rectal adenocarcinomas, 5% to 10% of the tumors have mismatch repair deficiency or high microsatellite instability. Immune checkpoint inhibitors are efficacious as a first-line therapy for metastatic colorectal cancers, with overall response rates of 30% to 60%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

27543</a>

2755-<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

27565</a>

2757] These responses proved durable, and prolonged overall survival (OS) was demonstrated in these settings.</p>

2758<p id="_1314" tabindex="-1">

2759Evidence (immunotherapy):</p>

2760<div class="pdq-content-list">

2761<ol id="_1315">

2762<li>

2763A phase II trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT04165772">

2764NCT04165772</a>

2765) studied dostarlimab, an anti-programmed death-1 (PD-1) monoclonal antibody, in 12 patients with locally advanced, mismatch repair&#8211;deficient, stage II or stage III rectal adenocarcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

27666</a>

2767]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2768<ul id="_1316">

2769<li>

2770 All 12 patients had clinical complete responses of 100% (95% confidence interval, 74%&#8211;100%) after a median follow-up of 12 months. Patients&#8217; cancers did not recur when the follow-up period ranged from 6 to 25 months. At the time of follow-up, chemoradiation therapy and surgery had been avoided.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

27716</a>

2772][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2773Level of evidence C3</a>

2774]</li>

2775<li>

2776Before this approach becomes a new standard, more patients need to be evaluated. A longer follow-up period is required to ensure durability and assess the need for future surgery or chemoradiation therapy.</li>

2777</ul>

2778</div>

2779</li>

2780</ol>

2781</div>

2782</section>

2783<section id="_550">

2784<h3 id="_550_toc">

2785Primary Surgical Therapy</h3>

2786<p id="_646" tabindex="-1">

2787The primary treatment for patients with rectal cancer is surgical resection of the primary tumor. The surgical approach to treatment varies according to the following:</p>

2788<div class="pdq-content-list">

2789<ul id="_857">

2790<li>

2791Tumor location.</li>

2792<li>

2793Stage of disease.</li>

2794<li>

2795Presence or absence of high-risk features (i.e., positive margins, lymphovascular invasion, perineural invasion, and poorly differentiated histology).</li>

2796</ul>

2797</div>

2798<p id="_858" tabindex="-1">

2799Types of surgical resection include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

28001</a>

2801,<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

28022</a>

2803,<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

28047</a>

2805]</p>

2806<div class="pdq-content-list">

2807<ul id="_647">

2808<li>

2809Polypectomy for select T1 cancers.</li>

2810<li>

2811Transanal local excision and transanal endoscopic microsurgery for select clinically staged T1/T2 N0 rectal cancers. </li>

2812<li>

2813Total mesorectal excision with autonomic nerve preservation techniques via low-anterior resection. </li>

2814<li>

2815Total mesorectal excision via abdominoperineal resection for patients who are not candidates for sphincter-preservation, leaving patients with a permanent end-colostomy. </li>

2816</ul>

2817</div>

2818<p id="_552" tabindex="-1">

2819Polypectomy alone may be used in certain instances (T1) in which polyps with invasive cancer can be completely resected with clear margins and have favorable histological features.[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

28208</a>

2821,<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

28229</a>

2823]</p>

2824<p id="_859" tabindex="-1">

2825Local excision of clinical T1 tumors is an acceptable surgical technique for appropriately selected patients. For all other tumors, a mesorectal excision is the treatment of choice. Very select patients with T2 tumors may be candidates for local excision. Local failure rates in the range of

28264% to 8% after rectal resection with appropriate mesorectal excision (total mesorectal excision for low/middle rectal tumors and mesorectal excision at

2827least 5 cm below the tumor for high rectal tumors) have been

2828reported.[<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

282910</a>

2830-<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

283114</a>

2832]</p>

2833<p id="_648" tabindex="-1">

2834 For patients with advanced cancers of the mid- to upper rectum, low-anterior resection followed by the creation of a colorectal anastomosis may be the treatment of choice. For locally advanced rectal cancers for which radical resection is indicated, however, total mesorectal excision with autonomic nerve preservation techniques via low-anterior resection is preferable to abdominoperineal resection.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

28351</a>

2836,<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

28372</a>

2838]</p>

2839<p id="_553" tabindex="-1">

2840The low incidence of local relapse after meticulous

2841mesorectal excision has led some investigators to question the routine use of

2842adjuvant radiation therapy. Because of an increased tendency for first failure in locoregional sites only,

2843the impact of perioperative radiation therapy is greater in rectal cancer than in

2844colon cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

284515</a>

2846]</p>

2847</section>

2848<section id="_649">

2849<h3 id="_649_toc">

2850Chemoradiation Therapy</h3>

2851<section id="_860">

2852<h4 id="_860_toc">

2853Preoperative chemoradiation therapy</h4>

2854<p id="_861" tabindex="-1">

2855Neoadjuvant therapy for rectal cancer, using preoperative chemoradiation therapy, is the preferred treatment option for patients with stages II and III disease. However, postoperative chemoradiation therapy for patients with stage II or III rectal cancer remains an acceptable option.[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

285616</a>

2857][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2858Level of evidence A1</a>

2859] </p>

2860<p id="_862" tabindex="-1">

2861Preoperative chemoradiation therapy has become the standard of care for patients with clinically staged T3&#8211;T4 or node-positive disease (stages II/III), based on the results of several studies:</p>

2862<div class="pdq-content-list">

2863<ul id="_863">

2864<li>

2865German Rectal Cancer Study Group trial.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

286617</a>

2867]</li>

2868<li>

2869National Surgical Adjuvant Breast and Bowel Project R-03 trial <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00410579">

2870NSABP R-03</a>

2871 (NCT00410579).[<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

287218</a>

2873][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2874Level of evidence A1</a>

2875] For more information, see the <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/rectal-treatment-pdq#_69">

2876Treatment of Stages II and III Rectal Cancer</a>

2877 section.</li>

2878</ul>

2879</div>

2880<p id="_865" tabindex="-1">

2881Multiple phase II and III studies examined the benefits of preoperative chemoradiation therapy, which include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

288216</a>

2883]</p>

2884<div class="pdq-content-list">

2885<ul id="_866">

2886<li>

2887Tumor regression and downstaging of the tumor.</li>

2888<li>

2889Improved tumor resectability.</li>

2890<li>

2891Higher rate of local control.</li>

2892<li>

2893Improved toxicity profile of chemoradiation therapy.</li>

2894<li>

2895Higher rate of sphincter preservation.</li>

2896</ul>

2897</div>

2898<p id="_867" tabindex="-1">

2899Complete pathological response rates of 10% to 25% may be achieved with preoperative chemoradiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

290019</a>

2901-<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

290226</a>

2903] However, preoperative radiation therapy is associated with increased complications compared with surgery alone. Some patients with cancers at a lower risk of local recurrence might be adequately treated with surgery and adjuvant chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

290427</a>

2905-<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

290630</a>

2907] For more information about these studies, see the <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/rectal-treatment-pdq#_706">

2908Preoperative chemoradiation therapy</a>

2909 section in the Treatment of Stages II and III Rectal Cancer section.</p>

2910</section>

2911<section id="_868">

2912<h4 id="_868_toc">

2913Postoperative chemoradiation therapy</h4>

2914<p id="_869" tabindex="-1">

2915Preoperative chemoradiation therapy is the current standard of care for stages II and III rectal cancer. However, before 1990, the following studies noted an increase in both disease-free survival (DFS) and OS with the use of postoperative combined-modality therapy:</p>

2916<div class="pdq-content-list">

2917<ol id="_871">

2918<li>

2919The Gastrointestinal Tumor

2920Study Group trial (GITSG-7175).</li>

2921<li>

2922The Mayo/North Central Cancer Treatment

2923Group trial (NCCTG-794751).</li>

2924<li>

2925The National Surgical Adjuvant Breast and

2926Bowel Project trial (NSABP-R-01).</li>

2927</ol>

2928</div>

2929<p id="_872" tabindex="-1">

2930Subsequent studies have attempted to increase the survival benefit by improving

2931radiation sensitization and by identifying the optimal chemotherapeutic agents

2932and delivery systems. </p>

2933<p id="_874" tabindex="-1">

2934<strong>

2935Fluorouracil (5-FU):</strong>

2936 The following studies examined optimal delivery methods for adjuvant 5-FU:</p>

2937<div class="pdq-content-list">

2938<ol id="_875">

2939<li>

2940Intergroup protocol 86-47-51 trial (MAYO-864751).[<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

294131</a>

2942][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2943Level of evidence A1</a>

2944] </li>

2945<li>

2946 Intergroup 0114 trial (INT-0114 [CLB-9081]).[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

294729</a>

2948][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2949Level of evidence A1</a>

2950] </li>

2951<li>

2952Intergroup 0144.[<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

295332</a>

2954]</li>

2955</ol>

2956</div>

2957<p id="_876" tabindex="-1">

2958For detailed information about these study results, see the <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/rectal-treatment-pdq#_69">

2959Treatment of Stages II and III Rectal Cancer</a>

2960 section.</p>

2961<p id="_877" tabindex="-1">

2962Acceptable

2963postoperative chemoradiation therapy for patients with stage II or III rectal cancer not

2964enrolled in clinical trials includes continuous-infusion 5-FU during 45 Gy to

296555 Gy pelvic radiation and four cycles of adjuvant maintenance chemotherapy with

2966bolus 5-FU with or without modulation with leucovorin (LV).

2967</p>

2968<p id="_878" tabindex="-1">

2969Findings from the NSABP-R-01 trial compared surgery alone with surgery followed by chemotherapy or radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

297033</a>

2971] Subsequently, the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00410579">

2972NSABP-R-02</a>

2973 study (NCT00410579), addressed whether adding postoperative radiation therapy to

2974chemotherapy would enhance the survival advantage reported in R-01.[<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

297534</a>

2976][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2977Level of evidence A1</a>

2978]</p>

2979<p id="_879" tabindex="-1">

2980In the NSABP-R-02 study, the addition of radiation therapy significantly reduced local recurrence at 5 years (8% for chemotherapy and radiation vs. 13% for chemotherapy alone, <em>

2981P</em>

2982 = .02) but failed to demonstrate a significant survival benefit. Radiation therapy appeared to improve survival among patients

2983younger than 60 years and among patients who underwent abdominoperineal resection.</p>

2984<p id="_881" tabindex="-1">

2985While this trial has initiated discussion in the oncologic community about the proper role of postoperative radiation therapy, omission of

2986radiation therapy seems premature because of the serious complications of locoregional recurrence.</p>

2987</section>

3179<section id="_883">

3180<h4 id="_883_toc">

3181Treatment toxicity</h4>

3182<p id="_884" tabindex="-1">

3183The acute side effects of pelvic radiation therapy for rectal cancer are mainly the result of gastrointestinal toxicity, are self-limiting, and usually resolve within 4 to 6 weeks of completing treatment. </p>

3184<p id="_885" tabindex="-1">

3185Of greater concern is the potential for late morbidity after rectal cancer treatment. Patients who undergo aggressive surgical procedures for rectal cancer can have chronic symptoms, particularly if there is impairment of the anal sphincter.[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

318635</a>

3187] Patients treated with radiation therapy appear to have increased chronic bowel dysfunction, anorectal sphincter dysfunction (if the sphincter was surgically preserved), and sexual dysfunction than do patients who undergo surgical resection alone.[<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

318828</a>

3189,<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

319036</a>

3191-<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

319241</a>

3193]</p>

3194<p id="_886" tabindex="-1">

3195An analysis of patients treated with postoperative chemotherapy and radiation

3196therapy suggests that these patients may have more chronic bowel dysfunction

3197than do patients who undergo surgical resection alone.[<a href="#cit/section_4.42">

319842</a>

3199] A Cochrane review highlights the risks of increased surgical morbidity as well as late rectal and sexual function in association with radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

320035</a>

3201] </p>

3202<p id="_888" tabindex="-1">

3203 Improved radiation therapy planning and techniques may minimize these acute and late treatment-related complications. These techniques include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.43">

320443</a>

3205-<a href="#cit/section_4.47">

320647</a>

3207]</p>

3208<div class="pdq-content-list">

3209<ul id="_889">

3210<li>

3211The use of high-energy radiation machines.</li>

3212<li>

3213The use of multiple pelvic radiation fields.</li>

3214<li>

3215Prone patient positioning.</li>

3216<li>

3217Customized patient molds (belly boards) to exclude as much small bowel as possible from the radiation fields and immobilize patients during treatment.</li>

3218<li>

3219Bladder distention during radiation therapy to exclude as much small bowel as possible from the radiation fields.</li>

3220<li>

3221Visualization of the small bowel through oral contrast during treatment planning so that, when possible, the small bowel can be excluded from the radiation field.</li>

3222<li>

3223The use of 3-dimensional or other advanced radiation planning techniques.</li>

3224</ul>

3225</div>

3226<p id="_890" tabindex="-1">

3227In Europe, it is common to deliver preoperative radiation therapy alone in one week (5 Gy&#160;&#215; five daily treatments) followed by surgery one week later, rather than the long-course chemoradiation approach used in the United States. One reason for this difference is the concern in the United States for heightened late effects when high radiation doses per fraction are given.</p>

3228<p id="_891" tabindex="-1">

3229A Polish study randomly assigned 316 patients to preoperative long-course chemoradiation therapy (50.4 Gy in 28 daily fractions with 5-FU/LV) or short-course preoperative radiation therapy (25 Gy in 5 fractions).[<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

323041</a>

3231]

3232Although the primary end point was sphincter preservation, late toxicity was not statistically significantly different between the two treatment approaches (7% long course vs. 10% short course). Of note, data on anal sphincter and sexual function were not reported, and toxicity was physician determined, not patient reported. </p>

3233<p id="_892" tabindex="-1">

3234Ongoing clinical trials comparing preoperative and postoperative adjuvant chemoradiation therapy should further clarify the impact of either approach on bowel function and other important quality-of-life issues (e.g., sphincter preservation) in addition to the more conventional end points of DFS and OS. </p>

3235</section>

3236</section>

3421Koelbl O, Richter S, Flentje M: Influence of patient positioning on dose-volume histogram and normal tissue complication probability for small bowel and bladder in patients receiving pelvic irradiation: a prospective study using a 3D planning system and a radiobiological model. Int J Radiat Oncol Biol Phys 45 (5): 1193-8, 1999.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10613312&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=10613312&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3422[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

3423</li>

3424<li id="section_4.47">

3425Gunderson LL, Russell AH, Llewellyn HJ, et al.: Treatment planning for colorectal cancer: radiation and surgical techniques and value of small-bowel films. Int J Radiat Oncol Biol Phys 11 (7): 1379-93, 1985.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=4008294&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=4008294&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3427</li>

3452</ul>

3453</a>

3461<section id="_669">

3462<h3 id="_669_toc">

3463Treatment Options for Stage 0 Rectal Cancer</h3>

3464<p id="_694" tabindex="-1">

3465Stage 0 rectal cancer or carcinoma <em>

3466in situ</em>

3467 is the most superficial of all rectal lesions and is limited

3468to the mucosa without invasion of the lamina propria.</p>

3469<p id="_670" tabindex="-1">

3470Treatment options for stage 0 rectal cancer include the following:</p>

3471<div class="pdq-content-list">

3472<ol id="_844">

3473<li>

3474<a href="/types/colorectal/hp/rectal-treatment-pdq#_673">

3475Polypectomy or surgery</a>

3476.</li>

3477</ol>

3478</div>

3479<section id="_673">

3480<h4 id="_673_toc">

3481Polypectomy or surgery</h4>

3482<p id="_674" tabindex="-1">

3483Local excision or simple polypectomy may be indicated for stage 0 rectal cancer tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

34841</a>

3485] Because of its localized nature at presentation, stage 0 rectal cancer has a high cure rate. For large lesions not amenable to local excision, full-thickness rectal resection by the transanal or transcoccygeal route may be performed.</p>

3486</section>

3487</section>

3494 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. 3495General information</a>

3496 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

3516

3517 <div id="_section_61" class="pdq-sections">

3526<a href="#_677_toc">

3529</ul>

3530</a>

3538<section id="_62">

3539<h3 id="_62_toc">

3540Treatment Options for Stage I Rectal Cancer</h3>

3541<p id="_695" tabindex="-1">

3542Stage I tumors extend beneath the mucosa into the submucosa (T1) or into, but not through, the bowel muscle wall (T2). Because of its localized nature at presentation, stage I rectal cancer has a high cure rate. </p>

3543<p id="_696" tabindex="-1">

3544Treatment options for stage I rectal cancer include the following:</p>

3545<div class="pdq-content-list">

3546<ol id="_697">

3547<li>

3548<a href="/types/colorectal/hp/rectal-treatment-pdq#_677">

3549Surgery with or without chemoradiation therapy</a>

3550.</li>

3551</ol>

3552</div>

3553<section id="_677">

3554<h4 id="_677_toc">

3555Surgery with or without chemoradiation therapy</h4>

3556<p id="_698" tabindex="-1">

3557There are three potential options for surgical resection in stage I rectal cancer: </p>

3558<div class="pdq-content-list">

3559<ul id="_897">

3560<li>

3561<strong>

3562Local excision.</strong>

3563 Local excision is restricted to tumors that are confined to the rectal wall and that do not, on rectal ultrasound or magnetic resonance imaging, involve the full thickness of the rectum (i.e., are not T3 tumors). The ideal candidate for local excision has a T1 tumor with well-to-moderate differentiation that occupies less than one-third of the circumference of the bowel wall. Local excision is associated with a higher risk of local and systemic failure and is applicable only to select patients with T2 tumors. Local transanal or other resection [<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

35641</a>

3565,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

35662</a>

3567] with or without perioperative

3568external-beam radiation therapy (EBRT) plus fluorouracil (5-FU) may be indicated.</li>

3569<li>

3570<strong>

3571Low-anterior

3572resection.</strong>

3573 Wide surgical resection and anastomosis are options when an adequate low-anterior

3574resection can be performed with sufficient distal rectum to allow a

3575conventional anastomosis or coloanal anastomosis. </li>

3576<li>

3577<strong>

3578Abdominoperineal resection.</strong>

3579 Wide surgical resection with abdominoperineal resection is used for lesions

3580too distal to permit low-anterior

3581resection. </li>

3582</ul>

3583</div>

3584<p id="_896" tabindex="-1">

3585Patients with tumors that are pathologically T1 may not need

3586postoperative therapy. Patients with tumors that are T2 or greater have lymph

3587node involvement about 20% of the time. Patients may want to consider additional therapy, such as

3588radiation therapy and chemotherapy, or wide surgical resection of the rectum.[<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

35893</a>

3590] Patients

3591with poor histological features or positive margins after local excision may consider low-anterior

3592resection or abdominoperineal resection and postoperative

3593treatment as dictated by full surgical staging.</p>

3594<p id="_703" tabindex="-1">

3595For patients with T1 and T2 tumors, no randomized

3596trials are available to compare local excision with or without postoperative chemoradiation

3597therapy to wide surgical resection (low-anterior

3598resection and abdominoperineal resection).</p>

3599<p id="_704" tabindex="-1">

3600Evidence (surgery):</p>

3601<div class="pdq-content-list">

3602<ol id="_1100">

3603<li>

3604Investigators with the Cancer and Leukemia Group B enrolled patients with T1 and T2 rectal adenocarcinomas that were within 10 cm of the dentate line and not more than 4 cm in diameter, and involving not more than 40% of the rectal circumference, onto a prospective protocol, CLB-8984. Patients with T1 tumors received no additional treatment after surgery, whereas patients with T2 tumors were treated with EBRT (54 Gy in 30 fractions, 5 days/week) and 5-FU (500 mg/m<span class="sup">

36052</span>

3606 on days 1 through 2 and days 29 through 31 of radiation therapy).[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

36074</a>

3608]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3609<ul id="_1021">

3610<li>

3611For patients with T1 tumors, at 48 months median follow-up, the 6-year failure-free survival (FFS) rate was 83%, and the overall survival (OS) rate was 87%. </li>

3612<li>

3613For patients with T2 tumors, the 6-year FFS rate was 71%, and the OS rate was 85%.</li>

3614</ul>

3615</div>

3616</li>

3617</ol>

3618</div>

3619</section>

3620</section>

3626advanced clinical trial search</a>

3627 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3628General information</a>

3629 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

Surgery 111 (5): 555-61, 1992.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=1598675&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=1598675&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3707<section id="_71">

3708<h3 id="_71_toc">

3709Treatment Options for Stages II and III Rectal Cancer</h3>

3710<p id="_689" tabindex="-1">

3711Treatment options for stages II and III rectal cancer include the following:</p>

3712<div class="pdq-content-list">

3713<ol id="_690">

3714<li>

3717.</li>

3718<li>

3719<a href="/types/colorectal/hp/rectal-treatment-pdq#_1088">

3720Short-course preoperative radiation therapy followed by surgery and chemotherapy</a>

3721.</li>

3722<li>

3733.</li>
