3736Immunotherapy</a>

3737 (for patients with mismatch repair deficiency or high microsatellite instability [MSI]).</li>

3738</ol>

3739</div>

3740<section id="_706">

3741<h4 id="_706_toc">

3742Preoperative chemoradiation therapy</h4>

3743<p id="_707" tabindex="-1">

3744Preoperative chemoradiation therapy has become the standard of care for patients with clinically staged T3 or T4 or node-positive disease, based on the results of several studies. </p>

3745<p id="_708" tabindex="-1">

3746Evidence (preoperative chemoradiation therapy):</p>

3747<div class="pdq-content-list">

3748<ol id="_709">

3749<li>

3750The German Rectal Cancer Study Group (CAO/ARO/AIO-94 [Working Group of Surgical Oncology/Working Group of Radiation Oncology/Working Group of Medical Oncology of the Germany Cancer Society]) randomly assigned 823 patients with ultrasound-staged T3 or T4 or lymph node-positive rectal cancer to either preoperative chemoradiation therapy or postoperative chemoradiation therapy (50.4 Gy in 28 daily fractions to the tumor and pelvic lymph nodes concurrent with infusional fluorouracil [5-FU] 1,000 mg/m<span class="sup">

37512 </span>

3752 daily for 5 days during the first and fifth weeks of radiation therapy).[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

37531</a>

3754][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3755Level of evidence A1</a>

3756] All patients underwent total mesorectal excision and received four additional cycles of 5-FU&#8211;based chemotherapy. <div class="pdq-content-list">

3757<ul id="_841">

3758<li>

3759The 5-year overall survival (OS) rates were 76% for preoperative chemoradiation therapy and 74% for postoperative chemoradiation therapy (<em>

3760P</em>

3761 = .80). The 5-year cumulative incidence of local relapse was 6% for patients assigned to the preoperative chemoradiation therapy group and 13% for patients in the postoperative chemoradiation therapy group (<em>

3762P</em>

3763 = .006). </li>

3764<li>

3765Grade 3 or 4 acute toxic effects occurred in 27% of patients in the preoperative-treatment group and in 40% of patients in the postoperative-treatment group (<em>

3766P</em>

3767 = .001). The corresponding rates of long-term toxic effects were 14% and 24%, respectively (<em>

3768P</em>

3769 = .01).</li>

3770<li>

3771The same number of patients underwent abdominoperineal resection in each arm. However, among the 194 patients with tumors that were determined by the surgeon before randomization to require an abdominoperineal excision, a statistically significant increase in sphincter preservation was achieved among patients who received preoperative chemoradiation therapy (<em>

3772P</em>

3773 = .004). These results have now been updated with a median follow-up of 11 years.[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

37742</a>

3775]</li>

3776<li>

3777The 10-year OS was equivalent in both arms, (59.6% in the preoperative group vs. 59.9% in the postoperative group; <em>

3778P</em>

3779 = .85). However, a local control benefit persists among patients treated with preoperative chemoradiation therapy compared with patients treated with postoperative chemoradiation therapy (10-year cumulative incidence of local relapse: 7.1% in the preoperative group vs. 10.1% in the postoperative group; <em>

3780P</em>

3781 = .048). There were no significant differences detected for 10-year cumulative incidence of distant metastases or disease-free survival (DFS).[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

37822</a>

3783]</li>

3784<li>

3785Among the patients assigned to the postoperative chemoradiation therapy treatment arm, 18% actually had pathologically determined stage I disease and were overestimated by endorectal ultrasound to have T3 or T4 or N1 disease. A similar number of patients were possibly overtreated in the preoperative treatment group.</li>

3786</ul>

3787</div>

3788</li>

3789<li>

3790The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00410579">

3791NSABP R-03</a>

3792 trial (NCT00410579) similarly compared preoperative versus postoperative chemoradiation therapy for patients with clinically staged T3 or T4 or lymph node-positive rectal cancer. Chemotherapy consisted of 5-FU/leucovorin (LV) with 45 Gy in 25 fractions with a 5.4 Gy boost. Although the intended sample size was 900 patients, the study with 267 patients closed early because of poor accrual.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

37933</a>

3794][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3795Level of evidence A1</a>

3796]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3797<ul id="_842">

3798<li>

3799With a median follow-up of 8.4 years, preoperative chemoradiation therapy was found to confer a significant improvement in 5-year DFS (64.7% vs. 53.4% for postoperative patients; <em>

3800P</em>

3801 = .011).</li>

3802<li>

3803Similar to the German Rectal Cancer Study, there was no significant difference in OS between treatment arms (74.5% for preoperative chemoradiation therapy vs. 65.6% for postoperative chemoradiation therapy; <em>

3804P</em>

3805 =. 065).</li>

3806</ul>

3807</div>

3808</li>

3809</ol>

3810</div>

3811</section>

3812<section id="_1088">

3813<h4 id="_1088_toc">

3814Short-course preoperative radiation therapy followed by surgery and chemotherapy</h4>

3815<p id="_1089" tabindex="-1">

3816The use of short-course radiation therapy before surgery has been a standard approach in parts of Europe and Australia. </p>

3817<p id="_1095" tabindex="-1">

3818Evidence (short-course preoperative radiation therapy):</p>

3819<div class="pdq-content-list">

3820<ol id="_1090">

3821<li>

3822The use of short-course radiation therapy was evaluated in a randomized study in the Swedish Rectal Cancer Trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00337545">

3823NCT00337545</a>

3824).[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

38254</a>

3826][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3827Level of evidence A1</a>

3828] In the trial, 1,168 patients younger than 80 years with stage I to stage III resectable rectal adenocarcinoma were randomly assigned to receive preoperative radiation therapy (25 Gy in five fractions) or to undergo immediate surgery. Patients did not receive adjuvant chemotherapy. <div class="pdq-content-list">

3829<ul id="_1101">

3830<li>

3831The 5-year OS rate was 58% in the radiation therapy group and 48% in the surgery group (<em>

3832P</em>

3833 = .005).</li>

3834<li>

3835The rate of local control was 11% in the radiation therapy group and 27% in the surgery group (<em>

3836P</em>

3837 &lt; .001). </li>

3838</ul>

3839</div>

3840<p id="_1102" tabindex="-1">

3841Subsequently, the Polish Rectal Trial and the Trans-Tasman Radiation Oncology Group (TROG) compared short-course preoperative radiation therapy with the standard long-course preoperative chemoradiation therapy administered with 5-FU.</p>

3842</li>

3843<li>

3844In the Polish Rectal Trial, 312 patients with clinical stage T3 or T4 rectal cancer were randomly assigned to receive preoperative radiation therapy (25 Gy in five fractions) followed by total mesorectal excision within 7 days, 6 months of adjuvant 5-FU/LV or preoperative chemoradiation therapy (50.4 Gy in 28 fractions with concurrent bolus 5-FU/LV), total mesorectal excision in 4 to 6 weeks after completion of radiation therapy, and 4 months of adjuvant 5-FU/LV.[<a href="#cit/section_7.5">

38455</a>

3846] The primary end point of the study was to detect a difference of at least 15% in sphincter preservation with a power of 80%. <div class="pdq-content-list">

3847<ul id="_1096">

3848<li>

3849 The rates of sphincter preservation were 61.2% in the short-course group and 58% in the chemoradiation therapy group (<em>

3850P</em>

3851 = .570). </li>

3852<li>

3853The actuarial 4-year survival rate was 67.2% in the short-course group and 66.2% in the chemoradiation therapy group (hazard ratio [HR], 1.01; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.69&#8211;1.48; <em>

3854P</em>

3855 = .960).</li>

3856<li>

3857The HR for local recurrence in the short-course group compared with the chemoradiation therapy group was 0.65 (95% CI, 0.32&#8211;1.28; <em>

3858P</em>

3859 = .210).</li>

3860<li>

3861There was no difference in late toxicity between the short-course group and the chemoradiation therapy group. </li>

3862</ul>

3863</div>

3864</li>

3865<li>

3866In the TROG trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00145769">

3867TROG 01.04</a>

3868 [NCT00145769]), 326 patients with ultrasound-staged or magnetic resonance imaging (MRI)&#8211;staged T3, N0 to N2, M0 rectal adenocarcinoma within 12 cm from the anal verge were randomly assigned to receive short-course radiation therapy (25 Gy in five fractions) followed by surgery 3 to 7 days later or long-course chemoradiation therapy (50.4 Gy in 28 fractions with concurrent continuous infusional 5-FU) followed by surgery in 4 to 6 weeks. All patients received adjuvant chemotherapy (5-FU/LV) after surgery. The trial was designed to have 80% power to detect a 10% difference in local recurrence at 3 years with a two-sided test at the 5% level of significance.[<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

38696</a>

3870] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3871<ul id="_1097">

3872<li>

3873Cumulative incidence of local recurrence at 3 years was 7.5% for the short-course group and 4.4% for the long-course group (<em>

3874P</em>

3875 = .24).</li>

3876<li>

3877The OS rate at 5 years was 74% for the short-course group and 70% for the long-course group (HR, 1.12; 95% CI, 0.76&#8211;1.67; <em>

3878P</em>

3879 = .62).</li>

3880</ul>

3881</div>

3882</li>

3883<li>

3884The Medical Research Council of the United Kingdom and the National Cancer Institute of Canada built on the short-course experience and conducted a randomized study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00003422">

3885MRC CR07</a>

3886 and <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00003422">

3887NCIC-CTG C016</a>

3888 [NCT00003422]) that compared short-course preoperative radiation therapy with selective postoperative chemoradiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

38897</a>

3890] In the trial, 1,350 patients from 80 centers who had resectable rectal adenocarcinomas that were less than 15 cm from the anal verge were randomly assigned. Of note, pelvic MRI or ultrasound was not mandated. Patients randomly assigned to short-course radiation therapy received 25 Gy in five fractions followed by total mesorectal excision and then adjuvant chemotherapy according to the local center policy about nodal and margin status. Patients randomly assigned to selective postoperative chemoradiation therapy received immediate surgery followed by postoperative chemoradiation (45 Gy in 25 fractions with concurrent 5-FU) if their circumferential resection margin was 1 mm or smaller. Adjuvant chemotherapy for the group that received selective chemoradiation therapy was again given on the basis of local standards regarding nodal and margin status.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

38917</a>

3892] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3893<ul id="_1103">

3894<li>

3895The risk of local recurrence at 3 years was 4.4% in the preoperative short-course group and 10.6% in the selective chemoradiation therapy group (HR, 0.39; 95% CI, 0.27&#8211;0.58; <em>

3896P</em>

3897 &lt; .0001). </li>

3898<li>

3899OS did not differ between the groups.</li>

3900</ul>

3901</div>

3902</li>

3903</ol>

3904</div>

3905<p id="_1092" tabindex="-1">

3906Taken together, these studies demonstrate that short-course preoperative radiation therapy and long-course preoperative chemoradiation therapy are both reasonable treatment strategies for patients with stage II or III rectal adenocarcinoma.</p>

3907</section>

3908<section id="_712">

3909<h4 id="_712_toc">

3910Postoperative chemoradiation therapy</h4>

3911<p id="_902" tabindex="-1">

3912Progress in the development of postoperative treatment regimens relates to the

3913integration of systemic chemotherapy and radiation therapy, as well as redefining the

3914techniques for both modalities. The efficacy of postoperative radiation therapy and

39155-FU-based chemotherapy for stages II and III rectal cancer was established by a

3916series of prospective, randomized clinical trials, including the following:[<a href="#cit/section_7.8">

39178</a>

3918-<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

391910</a>

3920][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3921Level of evidence A1</a>

3922] </p>

3923<div class="pdq-content-list">

3924<ul id="_903">

3925<li>

3926Gastrointestinal Tumor

3927Study Group (GITSG-7175).</li>

3928<li>

3929Mayo/North Central Cancer Treatment

3930Group (NCCTG-794751).</li>

3931<li>

3932National Surgical Adjuvant Breast and

3933Bowel Project (NSABP-R-01).</li>

3934</ul>

3935</div>

3936<p id="_904" tabindex="-1">

3937These studies

3938demonstrated an increase in DFS interval and OS

3939when radiation therapy was combined with chemotherapy after surgical

3940resection. After the publication in 1990 of the results of these trials, experts at a National Cancer

3941Institute-sponsored Consensus Development Conference recommended

3942postoperative combined-modality treatment for patients with

3943stages II and III rectal carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.11">

394411</a>

3945]

3946Since that time, preoperative chemoradiation therapy has become the standard of care, although postoperative chemoradiation therapy is still an acceptable alternative. For more information, see the <a href="/types/colorectal/hp/rectal-treatment-pdq#_706">

3947Preoperative chemoradiation therapy</a>

3948 section.</p>

3949<p id="_714" tabindex="-1">

3950Additional evidence (postoperative chemoradiation therapy):</p>

3951<div class="pdq-content-list">

3952<ol id="_715">

3953<li>

3954Intergroup protocol 86-47-51 (MAYO-864751) compared continuous-infusion 5-FU (225 mg/m<span class="sup">

39552</span>

3956/day throughout the entire course of radiation therapy) with bolus 5-FU (500 mg/m<span class="sup">

39572</span>

3958/day for 3 consecutive days during the first and fifth weeks of radiation therapy).[<a href="#cit/section_7.12">

395912</a>

3960][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3961Level of evidence A1</a>

3962] <div class="pdq-content-list">

3963<ul id="_905">

3964<li>

3965A 10% improvement in OS was demonstrated with the use of continuous-infusion 5-FU.</li>

3966</ul>

3967</div>

3968</li>

3969<li>

3970A three-arm randomized trial determined whether continuous-infusion 5-FU given throughout the entire standard six-cycle course of adjuvant chemotherapy was more effective than continuous infusion 5-FU given only during pelvic radiation therapy. Median follow-up was 5.7 years.[<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

397113</a>

3972]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3973<ol id="_1067" class="lower-alpha">

3974<li>

3975Arm 1 received bolus 5-FU in two 5-day cycles before (500 mg/m<span class="sup">

39762</span>

3977/day) and after (450 mg/m<span class="sup">

39782</span>

3979/day) radiation therapy, with protracted venous infusion 5-FU (225 mg/m<span class="sup">

39802</span>

3981/day) during radiation therapy.</li>

3982<li>

3983Arm 2 received continuous infusion 5-FU before (300 mg/m<span class="sup">

39842</span>

3985/day for 42 days), after (300 mg/m<span class="sup">

39862</span>

3987/day for 56 days), and during (225 mg/m<span class="sup">

39882</span>

3989/day) radiation therapy.</li>

3990<li>

3991Arm 3 received bolus 5-FU/LV in two 5-day cycles before (5-FU, 425 mg/m<span class="sup">

39922</span>

3993/day; LV, 20 mg/m<span class="sup">

39942</span>

3995/day) and after (5-FU, 380 mg/m<span class="sup">

39962</span>

3997/day; LV, 20 mg/m<span class="sup">

39982</span>

3999/day) radiation therapy, and bolus 5-FU/LV (5-FU, 400 mg/m<span class="sup">

40002</span>

4001/day; LV, 20 mg/m<span class="sup">

40022</span>

4003/day; days 1 to 4, every 28 days) during radiation therapy. Levamisole (150 mg/day) was administered in 3-day cycles every 14 days before and after radiation therapy.<div class="pdq-content-list">

4004<ul id="_1104">

4005<li>

4006No DFS, OS, or locoregional failure difference was detected (across all arms: 3-year DFS rate, 67%&#8211;69%; 3-year OS rate, 81%&#8211;83%; locoregional failure rate, 4.6%&#8211;8%).</li>

4007<li>

4008Lethal toxicity was less than 1%, with grades 3 to 4 hematologic toxicity in 55% of patients in arm 1 and in 49% of the patients in arm 3, versus in 4% of patients in the continuous-infusion arm.[<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

400913</a>

4010][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4011Level of evidence A1</a>

4012] </li>

4013</ul>

4014</div>

4015</li>

4016</ol>

4017</div>

4018</li>

4019<li>

4020 The final study results of Intergroup trial 0114 (INT-0114) showed no survival or local-control benefit with the addition of LV, levamisole, or both to 5-FU administered postoperatively for patients with stages II and III rectal cancers at a median follow-up of 7.4 years.[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

402114</a>

4022][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4023Level of evidence A1</a>

4024] </li>

4025<li>

4026A pooled analysis of 3,791 patients enrolled in clinical trials demonstrated that, for patients with T3, N0 disease, the 5-year OS rate with surgery plus chemotherapy (OS, 84%) compared favorably with the survival rates of patients treated with surgery plus radiation therapy and bolus chemotherapy (OS rate, 76%) or surgery plus radiation therapy and protracted-infusion chemotherapy (OS rate, 80%).[<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

402715</a>

4028]</li>

4029</ol>

4030</div>

4031</section>

4032<section id="_1022">

4033<h4 id="_1022_toc">

4034Surgery</h4>

4035<p id="_1023" tabindex="-1">

4036Total mesorectal excision with either low anterior resection or abdominoperineal resection is usually performed for stages II and III rectal cancer before or after chemoradiation therapy. </p>

4037<p id="_1024" tabindex="-1">

4038Retrospective studies have demonstrated that some patients with pathological T3, N0 disease treated with surgery and no additional therapy have a very low risk of local and systemic recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_7.16">

403916</a>

4040]</p>

4041</section>

4042<section id="_1216">

4043<h4 id="_1216_toc">

4044Primary chemoradiation therapy followed by intensive surveillance for complete clinical responders</h4>

4045<p id="_1217" tabindex="-1">

4046Since the advent of preoperative chemoradiation therapy in rectal cancer, the standard approach has been to recommend definitive surgical resection by either abdominoperineal resection or laparoscopic-assisted resection. In most series, after long-course chemoradiation therapy, 10% to 20% of patients will have a complete clinical response in which there is no sign of persistent cancer by imaging, rectal exam, or direct visualization during sigmoidoscopy. It was a long-held belief that most patients who did not undergo surgery for personal or medical reasons would experience a local and/or systemic recurrence. However, it became clear that patients with a pathological complete response to preoperative chemoradiation therapy followed by definitive surgery had a better DFS than did patients who did not have a pathological clinical response.[<a href="#cit/section_7.17">

404717</a>

4048] </p>

4049<p id="_1224" tabindex="-1">

4050Several single-institution studies have challenged this standard of care by demonstrating that most patients with complete clinical response will be cured of rectal cancer without surgery and that many patients who experience a local recurrence can be treated with surgical resection (abdominoperineal resection or laparoscopic-assisted resection) at the time of their recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_7.18">

405118</a>

4052-<a href="#cit/section_7.21">

405321</a>

4054] These institutional series were hampered by their small size and inherent selection bias.</p>

4055<p id="_1218" tabindex="-1">

4056Evidence (primary chemoradiation therapy followed by intensive surveillance for complete clinical responders):</p>

4057<div class="pdq-content-list">

4058<ol id="_1219">

4059<li>

4060Investigators in England performed the Oncological Outcomes after Clinical Complete Response in Patients with Rectal Cancer trial.[<a href="#cit/section_7.22">

406122</a>

4062] This was a propensity-score&#8722;matched cohort analysis. At a tertiary medical center in Manchester, 228 patients who chose watchful waiting from 2011 to 2013 after a complete clinical response to preoperative chemoradiation therapy were combined with 98 patients from a registry of three neighboring medical centers who chose watchful waiting after chemoradiation therapy beginning in 2005. A clinical complete response was considered in the absence of residual ulceration, stenosis, or mass within the rectum during digital rectal examination and endoscopic examination 8 weeks or more after completion of concurrent chemoradiation therapy. The only positive findings consistent with a complete clinical response during clinical or endoscopic examination were whitening of the mucosa and telangiectasia. Classification of complete clinical response required normal radiological imaging of the mesorectum and pelvis. Complete clinical responders (n = 129) were compared with a cohort of patients treated similarly who underwent surgery for complete resection (n = 228). Compared with all patients who underwent surgery, patients who chose watch and wait had tumors with an earlier T stage and N stage and that were less likely to be poorly differentiated. <div class="pdq-content-list">

4063<ul id="_1220">

4064<li>

4065After a median follow-up of 33 months, 44 (34%) of the 129 patients who chose watchful waiting had a local recurrence, and 36 patients had a salvage resection.</li>

4066<li>

4067In the paired-cohort analysis, the 3-year non-regrowth DFS rate for all patients was 83% (95% CI, 76%&#8211;88%): 88% (95% CI, 75%&#8211;94%) for the watch-and-wait group and 78% (95% CI, 63%&#8211;87%) for the surgical resection group (log-rank, <em>

4068P</em>

4069 = .022). </li>

4070<li>

4071The 3-year OS rate was 96% (95% CI, 88%&#8211;98%) in the watch-and-wait group versus 87% (95% CI, 77%&#8211;93%) for the surgical resection group (log-rank, <em>

4072P</em>

4073 = .015). </li>

4074<li>

4075The 3-year colostomy-free survival rate was 74% (95% CI, 64%&#8211;82%) for the watch-and-wait group and 47% (95% CI, 37%&#8211;57%; log-rank, <em>

4076P</em>

4077 &lt; .0001) for the surgical group.</li>

4078</ul>

4079</div>

4080<p id="_1221" tabindex="-1">

4081Patients managed by watch and wait underwent a more intensive follow-up protocol consisting of outpatient digital rectal examination; MRI (every 4&#8211;6 months in the first 2 years); examination under anesthesia or endoscopy; computed tomography scan of the chest, abdomen, and pelvis; and at least two carcinoembryonic antigen measurements in the first 2 years. The optimal follow-up has not been determined.</p>

4082<p id="_1222" tabindex="-1">

4083For patients who have a complete clinical response to therapy, it is reasonable to consider a watch-and-wait approach with intensive surveillance instead of immediate surgical resection. </p>

4084</li>

4085</ol>

4086</div>

4087</section>

4088<section id="_1317">

4089<h4 id="_1317_toc">

4090Immunotherapy</h4>

4091<p id="_1318" tabindex="-1">

4092Among patients with rectal adenocarcinomas, 5% to 10% of the tumors have mismatch repair deficiency or high MSI. Immune checkpoint inhibitors are efficacious as a first-line therapy for metastatic colorectal cancers, with overall response rates of 30% to 60%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

409323</a>

4094-<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

409525</a>

4096] These responses proved durable, and prolonged OS was demonstrated in these settings.</p>

4097<p id="_1319" tabindex="-1">

4098Evidence (immunotherapy):</p>

4099<div class="pdq-content-list">

4100<ol id="_1320">

4101<li>

4102A phase II trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT04165772">

4103NCT04165772</a>

4104) studied dostarlimab, an anti-programmed death-1 (PD-1) monoclonal antibody, in 12 patients with locally advanced, mismatch repair&#8211;deficient, stage II or stage III rectal adenocarcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.26">

410526</a>

4106]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4107<ul id="_1330">

4108<li>

4109All 12 patients had clinical complete responses of 100% (95% CI, 74%&#8211;100%) after a median follow-up of 12 months. Patients&#8217; cancers did not recur when the follow-up period ranged from 6 to 25 months. At the time of follow-up, chemoradiation therapy and surgery had been avoided.[<a href="#cit/section_7.26">

411026</a>

4111][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4112Level of evidence C3</a>

4113]</li>

4114<li>

4115Before this approach becomes a new standard, more patients need to be evaluated. A longer follow-up period is required to ensure durability and assess the need for future surgery or chemoradiation therapy.</li>

4116</ul>

4117</div>

4118</li>

4119</ol>

4120</div>

4121</section>

4122<section id="_1044">

4123<h4 id="_1044_toc">

4124Chemotherapy regimens</h4>

4125<p id="_1045" tabindex="-1">

4126Many academic oncologists suggest that LV/5-FU/oxaliplatin (FOLFOX) be considered the standard for adjuvant chemotherapy in rectal cancer. However, there are no data about rectal cancer to support this consideration. FOLFOX has become the standard arm in the latest Intergroup study evaluating adjuvant chemotherapy in rectal cancer. An Eastern Cooperative Oncology Group trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00217737">

4127ECOG-E5202</a>

4128 [NCT00217737]) randomly assigned patients with stage II or III rectal cancer who received preoperative or postoperative chemoradiation therapy to receive 6 months of FOLFOX with or without bevacizumab, but this trial closed because of poor accrual. No efficacy data are available.</p>

4129<section id="_1046">

4130<h5 id="_1046_toc">

4131Preoperative oxaliplatin with chemoradiation therapy</h5>

4132<p id="_1054" tabindex="-1">

4133Oxaliplatin has also shown radiosensitizing properties in preclinical models.[<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

413427</a>

4135] Phase II studies that combined oxaliplatin with fluoropyrimidine-based chemoradiation therapy have reported pathological complete response rates ranging from 14% to 30%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.28">

413628</a>

4137-<a href="#cit/section_7.32">

413832</a>

4139] Data from multiple studies have demonstrated a correlation between rates of pathological complete response and end points including distant metastasis-free survival, DFS, and OS.[<a href="#cit/section_7.33">

414033</a>

4141-<a href="#cit/section_7.35">

414235</a>

4143]</p>

4144<p id="_1105" tabindex="-1">

4145There is no current role for off-trial use of concurrent oxaliplatin and radiation therapy in the treatment of patients with rectal cancer.</p>

4146<p id="_1055" tabindex="-1">

4147Evidence (preoperative oxaliplatin with chemoradiation therapy):</p>

4148<div class="pdq-content-list">

4149<ol id="_1056">

4150<li>

4151The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00227747">

4152ACCORD 12/0405-Prodige 2</a>

4153 trial (NCT00227747), which randomly assigned 598 patients with clinically staged T2 or T3 or resectable T4 rectal cancer accessible by digital rectal examination to either preoperative radiation therapy (45 Gy in 25 fractions over 5 weeks) with capecitabine (800 mg/m<span class="sup">

41542</span>

4155 twice daily 5 of every 7 days) or to a higher dose of radiation (50 Gy in 25 fractions over 5 weeks) with the same dose of capecitabine and oxaliplatin (50 mg/m<span class="sup">

41562</span>

4157 weekly). Total mesorectal excision was performed in 98% of both groups at a median interval of 6 weeks after chemoradiation therapy was completed.[<a href="#cit/section_7.36">

415836</a>

4159]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4160<ul id="_1057">

4161<li>

4162Pathological complete response was the primary end point (albeit never validated as a true surrogate of OS). A higher percentage of patients achieved a pathological complete response in the oxaliplatin-treated group (19.2% vs. 13.9%). However, the difference did not reach statistical significance (<em>

4163P</em>

4164 = .09).</li>

4165<li>

4166The rate of grade 3 or 4 toxicity was significantly higher in the oxaliplatin-treated group (25% vs. 11%; <em>

4167P</em>

4168 &lt; .001), and there was no difference in the rate of sphincter-sparing surgery (75% vs. 78%). </li>

4169</ul>

4170</div>

4171</li>

4172<li>

4173Similarly, the STAR-01 trial investigated the role of oxaliplatin combined with 5-FU chemoradiation therapy for locally advanced rectal cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_7.37">

417437</a>

4175][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4176Level of evidence A1</a>

4177] This Italian study randomly assigned 747 patients with resectable, locally advanced, clinically staged T3 or T4 and/or clinical N1 to N2 adenocarcinoma of the mid- to low-rectum to receive either continuous-infusion 5-FU with radiation therapy or to receive the same regimen in combination with oxaliplatin (60 mg/m<span class="sup">

41782</span>

4179). Although the primary end point was OS, a protocol-planned analysis of response to preoperative therapy has been preliminarily reported.<div class="pdq-content-list">

4180<ul id="_1058">

4181<li>

4182The rate of pathological complete response was equivalent at 16% in both arms (odds ratio, 0.98; 95% CI, 0.66&#8211;1.44; <em>

4183P</em>

4184 = .904).</li>

4185<li>

4186There was no difference noted in the rate of pathologically positive lymph nodes, tumor infiltration beyond the muscularis propria, or the rate of circumferential margin positivity. </li>

4187<li>

4188An increase in grades 3 to 4 treatment-related acute toxicity was noted with the addition of oxaliplatin (24% vs. 8%; <em>

4189P</em>

4190 &lt;.001). Longer-term outcomes including OS have not yet been reported.</li>

4191</ul>

4192</div>

4193</li>

4194<li>

4195The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00058474">

4196NSABP-R-04</a>

4197 trial (NCT00058474) randomly assigned 1,608 patients with clinically staged T3 or T4 or clinical node-positive adenocarcinoma within 12 cm of the anal verge in a 2 &#215; 2 factorial design to one of the following four treatment groups:<div class="pdq-content-list">

4198<ul id="_1331">

4199<li>

4200Intravenous (IV) continuous infusion 5-FU with radiation therapy.</li>

4201<li>

4202Capecitabine with radiation therapy.</li>

4203<li>

4204IV continuous infusion 5-FU plus weekly oxaliplatin with radiation therapy. </li>

4205<li>

4206Capecitabine plus weekly oxaliplatin with radiation therapy.</li>

4207</ul>

4208</div>

4209<p id="_1060" tabindex="-1">

4210The primary objective of this study is locoregional disease control.[<a href="#cit/section_7.38">

421138</a>

4212][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4213Level of evidence B1</a>

4214] Preliminary results, reported in abstract form at the 2011 American Society of Clinical Oncology annual meeting, demonstrated the following: </p>

4215<div class="pdq-content-list">

4216<ul id="_1061">

4217<li>

4218There was no significant difference in the rates of pathological complete response, sphincter-sparing surgery, or surgical downstaging between the 5-FU and capecitabine regimens or between the regimens with and without oxaliplatin. </li>

4219<li>

4220Patients treated with oxaliplatin had significantly higher rates of grade 3 and grade 4 acute toxicity (15.4% vs. 6.6%; <em>

4221P</em>

4222 &lt; .001).</li>

4223</ul>

4224</div>

4225</li>

4226<li>

4227The German CAO/ARO/AIO-04 trial randomly assigned 1,236 patients with clinically staged T3 to T4 or clinical lymph node-positive adenocarcinoma within 12 cm from the anal verge to receive either concurrent chemoradiation therapy with 5-FU (week 1 and week 5) or concurrent chemoradiation therapy with 5-FU daily (250 mg/m<span class="sup">

42282</span>

4229) and oxaliplatin (50 mg/m<span class="sup">

42302</span>

4231).[<a href="#cit/section_7.39">

423239</a>

4233][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4234Level of evidence B1</a>

4235]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4236<ul id="_1062">

4237<li>

4238In contrast to the previous studies, a significantly higher rate of pathological complete response was achieved in patients who received oxaliplatin (17% vs. 13%; <em>

4239P</em>

4240 = .038). </li>

4241<li>

4242There was no significant difference in rates of overall grades 3 and 4 toxicity; however, diarrhea and nausea and vomiting were more common among those treated with oxaliplatin.</li>

4243<li>

4244The 5-FU schedules in this study differed between the two arms, which may have contributed to the difference in outcomes noted. Longer follow-up will be necessary to determine the effect on the primary end point of the study, DFS.</li>

4245</ul>

4246</div>

4247</li>

4248</ol>

4249</div>

4250</section>

4251<section id="_1053">

4252<h5 id="_1053_toc">

4253Postoperative oxaliplatin-containing regimens</h5>

4254<p id="_1047" tabindex="-1">

4255On the basis of results of several studies, oxaliplatin as a radiation sensitizer does not appear to add any benefit in terms of primary tumor response, and it has been associated with increased acute treatment-related toxicity. The question of whether oxaliplatin should be added to adjuvant 5-FU/LV for postoperative management of stages II and III rectal cancer is an ongoing debate. There are no randomized phase III studies to support the use of oxaliplatin for the adjuvant treatment of rectal cancer. However, the addition of oxaliplatin to 5-FU/LV for the adjuvant treatment of colon cancer is now considered standard care.</p>

4256<p id="_1048" tabindex="-1">

4257Evidence (postoperative oxaliplatin):</p>

4258<div class="pdq-content-list">

4259<ol id="_1049">

4260<li>

4261In the randomized Multicenter International Study of Oxaliplatin/5-FU/LV in the Adjuvant Treatment of Colon Cancer (MOSAIC) study, the toxic effects and efficacy of FOLFOX4 (a 2-hour infusion of 200 mg/m<span class="sup">

42622</span>

4263 LV, followed by a bolus of 400 mg/m<span class="sup">

42642</span>

4265 5-FU, and then a 22-hour infusion of 600 mg/m<span class="sup">

42662</span>

4267 5-FU on 2 consecutive days every 14 days for 12 cycles, plus a 2-hour infusion of 85 mg/m<span class="sup">

42682</span>

4269 oxaliplatin on day 1, given simultaneously with LV) were compared with the same 5-FU/LV regimen without oxaliplatin when administered for 6 months. Each arm of the trial included 1,123 patients.[<a href="#cit/section_7.40">

427040</a>

4271]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4272<ol id="_1085" class="lower-alpha">

4273<li>

4274Preliminary results of the study, with 37 months of follow-up, demonstrated a significant improvement in DFS at 3 years in favor of FOLFOX4 (77.8% vs. 72.9%; <em>

4275P</em>

4276 = .01). When initially reported, there was no difference in OS.[<a href="#cit/section_7.41">

427741</a>

4278][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4279Level of evidence B1</a>

4280]</li>

4281<li>

4282Further follow-up at 6 years demonstrated that the OS for all patients (both stage II and stage III) entered into the study was not significantly different (OS rate, 78.5% FOLFOX4 vs. 76.0% 5-FU/LV group; HR, 0.84; 95% CI, 0.71&#8211;1.00).<div class="pdq-content-list">

4283<ul id="_1086">

4284<li>

4285On subset analysis, the 6-year OS rate in patients with stage III colon cancer was 72.9% in the patients who received FOLFOX4 and 68.9% in the patients who received 5-FU/LV (HR, 0.80; 95% CI, 0.65&#8211;0.97; <em>

4286P</em>

4287 = .023).[<a href="#cit/section_7.41">

428841</a>

4289][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4290Level of evidence A1</a>

4291]</li>

4292<li>

4293Patients treated with FOLFOX4 experienced more frequent toxic effects, consisting mainly of neutropenia (41% &gt; grade 3) and reversible peripheral sensory neuropathy (12.4% &gt; grade 3). </li>

4294</ul>

4295</div>

4296</li>

4297</ol>

4298</div>

4299</li>

4300<li>

4301The results of the completed NSABP-C-07 study confirmed and extended the results of the MOSAIC trial.[<a href="#cit/section_7.42">

430242</a>

4303] In NSABP C-07, 2,492 patients with stage II or III colon or rectal cancer were randomly assigned to receive either FLOX (2-hour IV infusion of 85 mg/m<span class="sup">

43042</span>

4305 oxaliplatin on days 1, 15, and 29 of each 8-week treatment cycle, followed by a 2-hour IV infusion of 500 mg/m<span class="sup">

43062</span>

4307 LV plus bolus 500 mg/m<span class="sup">

43082</span>

4309 5-FU 1 hour after the start of the LV infusion on days 1, 8, 15, 22, 29, and 36, followed by a 2-week rest period, for a total of three cycles [24 weeks]) or the same chemotherapy without oxaliplatin (Roswell Park regimen).<div class="pdq-content-list">

4310<ul id="_1052">

4311<li>

4312The 3- and 4-year DFS rates were 71.8% and 67% for the Roswell Park regimen and 76.1% and 73.2% for FLOX, respectively.</li>

4313<li>

4314The HR was 0.80 (95% CI, 0.69&#8211;0.93), a 20% risk reduction in favor of FLOX (<em>

4315P</em>

4316 &lt; .004).</li>

4317</ul>

4318</div>

4319</li>

4320</ol>

4321</div>

4322<p id="_1114" tabindex="-1">

4323It is unclear whether the results of these colon cancer trials can be applied to the management of patients with rectal cancer. There are no randomized phase III studies to support the routine practice of administering FOLFOX as adjuvant therapy to patients with rectal cancer.</p>
